<t0>I</t0>
<t1>THE NIGHT</t1>
<t2>CHAPTER 1</t2>
<t3>We slept in what had once been the gymnasium.</t3> <t4>The floor, made of varnished wood, had lines and circles painted on it for various sports.</t4> <t5>The basketball hoops were still there, but the nets had disappeared.</t5> <t6>The room was surrounded by a gallery for the audience; and I had the impression that I could perceive, like in a vague mirage, the acrid smell of sweat mixed with that sweet touch of chewing gum and the perfume of the girls who were among the audience, dressed in felt skirts — that's how I had seen them in the photos —, later in miniskirts, then in pants, finally with a single earring and styled with crests of green stripes.</t6> <t7>Dances had been held here; the music persisted, a palimpsest of sounds that no one listened to, one style after another, a background of drums, a melancholic wail, garlands of flowers made of tissue paper, cardboard demons, a rotating mirror ball that sprinkled the dancers with flakes of light.</t7>
<t8>In the room, there were reminiscences of sex and loneliness and expectation, the expectation of something without form or name.</t8> <t9>I remember that sensation, the longing for something that was always about to happen and was never the same, just as the hands that, without wasting time, caressed our lumbar region were not the same, or slipped between our clothes when we crouched in the parking lot or in the television room with the device muted and the images flickering over our exalted flesh.</t9>
<t10>We sighed for the future.</t10> <t11>Where did we get that talent for insatiability?</t11> <t12>It floated in the air; and it was still breathed in, like a late idea, when we tried to sleep on the army cots arranged in a row and separated from each other so we couldn't talk.</t12> <t13>We had cotton flannel sheets, like the ones children use, and army blankets, so old they still bore the initials U. S.</t13> <t14>We folded our clothes very neatly and left them on the stool at the foot of the bed.</t14> <t15>The lights would dim immediately but were never turned off.</t15> <t16>Aunt Sara and Aunt Elizabeth made the rounds; on their leather belts they carried electric prods like the ones used for cattle.</t16>
<t17>However, they did not carry weapons; not even those would have been entrusted to them.</t17> <t18>Their use was reserved for the Guardians, who were specially chosen from among the Angels.</t18> <t19>The presence of Guardians inside the building was not allowed, except when they were called; and we were not allowed to go out, except for our walks, twice a day and in pairs, around the football field that was now fenced with a chain-link fence topped with barbed wire.</t19> <t20>The Angels remained outside, turning their backs to us.</t20> <t21>For us, they were a source of fear, and also something more.</t21> <t22>If only they would look at us, if we could talk to them...</t22> <t23>We believed that we could exchange something, make a deal, reach an agreement, we still had our bodies...</t23> <t24>This was our fantasy.</t24>
<t25>We learned to whisper almost without making a sound.</t25> <t26>In the semi-darkness, when the Aunts weren't looking, we would stretch out our arms and touch each other's hands.</t26>
<t27>We learned to read the movement of the lips: with our heads pressed against the bed, lying on our sides, we watched each other's mouths.</t27> <t28>Thus, from one bed to another, we communicated the names: Alma, Janine, Dolores, Moira, June.</t28>
<t29>II</t29>
<t30>THE PURCHASE</t30>
<t31>CHAPTER 2</t31>
<t32>A chair, a table, a lamp.</t32> <t33>Above, on the white ceiling, a relief ornament in the shape of a garland, and in the center of it a blank space covered with plaster, like a face from which the eyes have been gouged out.</t33> <t34>There must have once been a chandelier there.</t34> <t35>But they have removed all the objects to which a rope could be tied.</t35>
<t36>A window, two white curtains.</t36> <t37>Under the window, a seat with a small cushion.</t37> <t38>When the window is partially opened –it only opens partially— air comes in and moves the curtains.</t38> <t39>I can sit in the chair, or on the window seat, with my hands crossed, and dedicate myself to contemplating.</t39> <t40>Sunlight also enters through the window and projects onto the narrow, highly waxed wooden floorboards.</t40>
<t41>I can smell the wax.</t41> <t42>On the floor, there is an oval rug, made from old braided rags.</t42> <t43>This is the kind of detail they like: folk art, archaic, made by women in their spare time with things that are no longer useful.</t43> <t44>A return to traditional values.</t44> <t45>Do not consume, do not desire.</t45> <t46>If I do not consume, why do I still desire?</t46>
<t47>On the wall, above the chair, a painting with a frame but without glass: it is a watercolor of flowers, of blue irises.</t47> <t48>Flowers are still allowed.</t48> <t49>I wonder if the others also have a painting, a chair, some white curtains.</t49> <t50>Are these items distributed by the government?</t50>
<t51>"Act as if you were in the army," said Aunt Lydia.</t51>
<t52>A bed.</t52> <t53>Single, with a semi-firm mattress covered by a white quilt filled with batting.</t53> <t54>In the bed, nothing is done except sleep... or not sleep.</t54> <t55>I try not to think too much.</t55> <t56>Like everything else, thought has to be rationed.</t56> <t57>There are many who cannot bear to think.</t57> <t58>Thinking can harm your chances, and I intend to resist.</t58> <t59>I know why the painting of the blue lilies has no glass, and why the window only opens partially, and why the window glass is shatterproof.</t59> <t60>What they fear is not that we might escape –after all, we wouldn't get very far— but those other exits, the ones you can open within yourself if you have a sharp mind.</t60>
<t61>So, apart from these details, this could be the guest room of a school, but the room for the less distinguished visitors; or a room in a guesthouse like the ones from before, suitable for ladies of limited means.</t61> <t62>This is where we are now.</t62> <t63>The possibilities have been reduced... for those of us who still have possibilities.</t63>
<t64>But the chair, the sunlight, the flowers... should not be despised.</t64> <t65>I am alive, I live, I breathe, I extend my open hand to the sunlight.</t65> <t66>The place where I am is not a prison but a privilege, as Aunt Lydia used to say, who loved extremes.</t66>
<t67>The bell that marks the time is ringing.</t67> <t68>Here, time is marked by bells, as it used to be in nunneries.</t68> <t69>And, also like in a convent, there are few mirrors.</t69>
<t70>I get up from the chair, take a step towards the sunlight with the red low-heeled shoes, designed to protect the spine but not for dancing.</t70> <t71>The red gloves are on the bed.</t71> <t72>I pick them up and put them on, finger by finger.</t72> <t73>Except for the headdress that surrounds my face, everything is red, the color of blood, which is what defines us.</t73>
<t74>The skirt is long down to the ankles and wide, gathered in a smooth yoke that covers the chest, and the sleeves are wide.</t74> <t75>The white headdress is mandatory; its purpose is to prevent us from seeing, and also from being seen.</t75> <t76>Red never suited me well, it's not my color.</t76> <t77>I grab the shopping basket and hang it on my arm.</t77>
<t78>The door of the room – not my room, I refuse to acknowledge it as mine</t78>
<t79>— it is not locked.</t79> <t80>In fact, it cannot even be adjusted.</t80> <t81>I step out into the hallway, waxed and covered with a central carpet of ashen pink color.</t81> <t82>Like a path in the forest, like a carpet for royalty, it shows me the way.</t82>
<t83>The carpet traces a curve and descends the stairs; I follow it, resting one hand on the banister that was once a tree, crafted in another century, polished to make it shine.</t83> <t84>The house is of late Victorian style and was built for a wealthy and large family.</t84> <t85>In the hallway, there is a pendulum clock that languidly marks the time, and then a door that leads to the maternal living room, populated with shadows.</t85> <t86>A room in which I never sit, I only stand or kneel.</t86> <t87>At the end of the hallway, above the front door, there is a fanlight with colored glass forming red and blue flowers.</t87>
<t88>On the wall of the living room, there is still a mirror.</t88> <t89>If I turn my head – in such a way that the white cap framing my face directs my vision towards it – I can see it while descending the stairs: a round, convex, full-body mirror, like a fish's eye, and my image reflected in it like a distorted shadow, a parody of something, like the figure from a fairy tale covered with a red cloak, descending towards a moment of indifference that is equal to danger.</t89> <t90>A sister, bathed in blood.</t90>
<t91>At the foot of the staircase, there is a coat rack for hats and umbrellas; it has long, rounded wooden bars that gently curve to form hooks, imitating the leaves of a fern.</t91> <t92>Several umbrellas hang from it: a black one for the Commander, a blue one for the Commander's Wife, and the one assigned to me, which is red.</t92> <t93>I leave the red umbrella in its place: through the window, I see the sun shining.</t93> <t94>I wonder if the Commander's Wife is in the living room.</t94> <t95>She is not always sitting there.</t95> <t96>Sometimes I hear her pacing back and forth, one strong step and then a soft one, and the dull tap of her cane on the ash-pink carpet.</t96>
<t97>I walk down the hallway, pass by the living room door and the one that leads to the dining room; I open the one at the end and enter the kitchen.</t97> <t98>It doesn't smell like waxed wood here.</t98>
<t99>I find Rita standing by the table painted with white enamel.</t99> <t100>She is wearing her usual Martha dress, of a dull green color, like a surgeon's gown from times past.</t100> <t101>The cut of her dress is very similar to mine, long and modest, but on top she wears an apron with a bib and has no cap or veil.</t101> <t102>She only wears the veil when going out, but no one cares too much who sees a Martha's face.</t102> <t103>Her dress is rolled up to her elbows, revealing her dark arms.</t103>
<t104>She is making bread, spreading the dough for the brief final kneading and to shape it.</t104>
<t105>Rita sees me and moves her head –it's hard to tell whether as a greeting or just becoming aware of my presence—; she wipes her flour-covered hands on her apron and rummages through the drawer in search of the voucher book.</t105>
<t106>He frowns, tears off three vouchers, and hands them to me.</t106> <t107>If he smiled, his face might appear kind.</t107> <t108>But his expression is not directed personally at me: he dislikes the red dress and what it represents.</t108> <t109>He thinks I might be contagious, like a disease or some kind of misfortune.</t109>
<t110>Sometimes I listen behind doors, something I would never have done before.</t110>
<t111>I don't listen for too long because I don't want to get caught.</t111> <t112>However, once I heard Rita telling Cora that she wouldn't lower herself in that way.</t112>
<t113>Nobody is asking you to, replied Cora.</t113> <t114>Anyway, what would you do if you could?</t114>
<t115>Going to the Colonies, stated Rita.</t115> <t116>They have an alternative.</t116>
<t117>With the No Women, starving to death and God knows what else?, asked Cora.</t117>
<t118>You are crazy.</t118>
<t119>They were shelling peas; even through the half-closed door, I could hear the clinking sound the peas made as they fell into the metal bowl.</t119> <t120>I heard Rita grunt or sigh, I don't know if in protest or approval.</t120>
<t121>Anyway, they do it for us, or so they say, continued Cora.</t121> <t122>If I didn't have my tubes tied, it could be me, if I were ten years younger.</t122> <t123>It's not that bad.</t123> <t124>It's not what you would call hard work.</t124>
<t125>She is better than me, said Rita, and at that moment I opened the door.</t125>
<t126>They had the expression that women have when you catch them talking about you behind your back and they think you’ve heard them: an expression of discomfort and at the same time defiance, as if they were within their rights.</t126> <t127>That day, Cora was kinder to me than usual and Rita more surly.</t127>
<t128>Today, despite Rita's impenetrable face and her tight lips, I would like to stay in the kitchen.</t128> <t129>Cora would come from somewhere else in the house with her bottle of lemon oil and her duster, and Rita would make coffee—in the Commanders' houses there is still real coffee—and we would sit around Rita's table—which belongs to her no more than mine belongs to me—and we would chat about ailments, illnesses, our feet, our backs, the different kinds of mischief that our bodies—as unruly creatures—</t129>
<t130>they are capable of committing.</t130> <t131>We would nod our heads, as if each nod punctuated the other's sentence, indicating that yes, we already know what it's about.</t131> <t132>We would exchange remedies and try to outdo each other in the recital of our physical miseries; we would lament quietly, in a low and sad voice, in a minor tone like the pigeons nesting in the gutters of buildings.</t132> <t133>I know what you mean, we would affirm.</t133> <t134>Or, using an expression still heard from the mouths of older people: I hear where you're coming from, as if the voice itself were a traveler arriving from some distant place.</t134> <t135>Which it could be, which it is.</t135>
<t136>I used to disdain this type of conversation.</t136> <t137>Now I desire it ardently.</t137> <t138>At least it is a conversation, a way to exchange something.</t138>
<t139>Or we would dedicate ourselves to gossiping.</t139> <t140>The Marthas know things, they talk among themselves and pass unofficial news from house to house.</t140> <t141>There is no doubt that they listen behind doors, like me, and see things despite those averted eyes.</t141> <t142>I have sometimes heard them, I have caught snippets of their private conversations.</t142> <t143>It was stillborn.</t143> <t144>Or: She stuck a knitting needle right into her belly.</t144> <t145>It must have been jealousy, which was devouring her.</t145> <t146>Or, in a tormenting tone: What she used was a cleaning product.</t146>
<t147>It worked like a charm, although anyone would say that he had tested it.</t147>
<t148>It must have been that drunkard; but they found her right away.</t148>
<t149>Or I would help Rita make the bread, sinking my hands into that soft and resilient warmth that resembles Flesh so much.</t149> <t150>I'm dying to touch something, something that isn't fabric or wood.</t150> <t151>I'm dying to commit the act of touching.</t151>
<t152>But even if they asked me, even if I breached decorum to that extent, Rita would not allow it.</t152> <t153>She would be too worried.</t153> <t154>The Marthas are not supposed to fraternize with us.</t154>
<t155>To fraternize means to behave like a brother.</t155> <t156>Luke told me that.</t156> <t157>He said there was no equivalent for behaving like a sister.</t157> <t158>According to him, it had to be sororize, from Latin.</t158> <t159>He liked knowing those kinds of details, the origins of words and their less common uses.</t159> <t160>I used to tease him for his pedantry.</t160>
<t161>I take the vouchers that Rita hands me.</t161> <t162>They have drawings of the foods they can be exchanged for: a dozen eggs, a piece of cheese, a brown thing that is supposed to be a steak.</t162> <t163>I put them in the zippered pocket of my sleeve, where I keep the pass.</t163>
<t164>—Tell them the eggs should be fresh —she warns me—.</t164> <t165>Not like last time.</t165> <t166>And make sure they give you a chicken, not a hen.</t166> <t167>Tell them who it's for and you'll see they won't mess it up.</t167>
<t168>—Agreed —I respond.</t168> <t169>I don't smile.</t169> <t170>Why tempt her with a friendly attitude?</t170>
<t171>CHAPTER 3</t171>
<t172>I go out through the back door to the garden, large and well-kept: in the middle, there is grass, a willow tree, and candlesticks; at the edges, flower beds: daffodils that are beginning to wither and tulips that are opening in a torrent of color.</t172> <t173>The tulips are red, and a darker crimson color near the stem, as if they had been wounded and were beginning to heal.</t173>
<t174>This garden is the realm of the Commander's Wife.</t174> <t175>Often, when I look out from my shatterproof glass window, I see her here, kneeling on a cushion, with a light blue veil over her large hat and beside her a basket with some scissors and pieces of thread to tie the flowers.</t175> <t176>The Guardian assigned.</t176> <t177>to the Commander is the one who performs the heavy task of digging the soil.</t177> <t178>The Commander's Wife directs the operation, pointing with her cane.</t178> <t179>Many Commanders' wives have gardens like this; that way they can give orders and keep themselves busy.</t179>
<t180>Once I had a garden.</t180> <t181>I remember the smell of the turned earth, the rounded shape of the opened bulbs, the dry crunch of the seeds between my fingers.</t181> <t182>That way, time passed more quickly.</t182> <t183>Sometimes the Commander's Wife takes a chair out to her garden and sits there.</t183> <t184>From a certain distance, she radiates a halo of peace.</t184>
<t185>Now she is not here, and I start to wonder where she might be: I don't like running into her by surprise.</t185> <t186>Perhaps she is sewing in the living room, with her arthritic left foot on the footstool.</t186> <t187>Or knitting scarves for the Angels who are on the front lines.</t187>
<t188>I find it hard to believe that the Angels need to wear those scarves; in any case, the Commander's Wife's scarves are very elaborate.</t188> <t189>She is not satisfied with the pattern of crosses and stars, like the other Wives, because it does not represent a challenge.</t189> <t190>At the ends of her scarves, fir trees, or eagles, or rigid humanoid figures parade: a boy, a girl, a boy, a girl.</t190> <t191>They are not scarves for adults but for children.</t191>
<t192>Sometimes I think that they are not sent to the Angels, but rather they are unraveled and turned back into balls of yarn to be woven again.</t192> <t193>Maybe it's only to keep them busy, to give meaning to their lives; but I envy the weaving of the Commander's Wife.</t193> <t194>It's good to have small, easily attainable goals.</t194>
<t195>And what does she envy about me?</t195>
<t196>She doesn't speak to me unless she can't avoid it.</t196> <t197>To her, I am a disgrace.</t197> <t198>And a necessity.</t198>
<t199>The first time we were face to face was five weeks ago, when I arrived at this detachment.</t199> <t200>The Guardian of the previous detachment accompanied me to the main door.</t200> <t201>The first few days we are allowed to use the main door, but afterwards we have to use the back ones.</t201> <t202>Things haven't stabilized, it's still too early and no one is sure of their exact situation.</t202> <t203>In a while, there will be nothing but main doors and back doors.</t203>
<t204>Aunt Lydia told me that she pressured them to let me use the front door.</t204>
<t205>Yours is a place of honor, he said.</t205>
<t206>The Guardian rang the bell for me, and the door opened immediately, in less time than it would take for someone to go and answer.</t206> <t207>Surely she was on the other side, waiting.</t207> <t208>I thought a Martha was going to appear, but instead, she came out, dressed in her unmistakable pale blue outfit.</t208>
<t209>So, you're the new one, she said to me.</t209> <t210>She didn't even move aside to let me in; she stayed in the doorway, blocking the entrance.</t210> <t211>She wanted me to realize that I couldn't enter the house unless she allowed it.</t211> <t212>These days, you always have the feeling that you're walking on a tightrope.</t212>
<t213>Yes, I replied.</t213>
<t214>Leave it on the porch, she told the Guardian, who was carrying my suitcase.</t214> <t215>It was made of red vinyl and not very large.</t215> <t216>I had another suitcase with my winter coat and thicker dresses, but they would bring it later.</t216>
<t217>The Guardian dropped the suitcase and greeted the Commander's Wife.</t217> <t218>Then I perceived his steps retracing the path, heard the click of the gate, and had the sensation of being stripped of a protective hand.</t218> <t219>The threshold of a new house is a desolate place.</t219>
<t220>She waited for the car to start and drive away.</t220> <t221>I didn't look at her face; I only looked at what I could perceive with my head down: her thick blue waist and her left hand on the ivory handle of her cane, the huge diamonds on the ring, which must have once been fine and still preserved well, the nail of a knobby finger filed into a smooth curve.</t221> <t222>It was as if that finger bore an ironic smile, as if it mocked her.</t222>
<t223>"You'd better come in," he said.</t223> <t224>He turned, giving me his back, and limped into the hallway.</t224> <t225>"And close the door."</t225>
<t226>I carried the red suitcase inside, as she surely wanted, and closed the door.</t226> <t227>I didn't say anything to her.</t227> <t228>Aunt Lydia said it was better not to speak unless you were asked a direct question.</t228> <t229>Try to put yourself in her place, she said, squeezing my hands and smiling with a nervous and pleading expression.</t229> <t230>It's not easy for them.</t230>
<t231>Here, said the Commander's Wife.</t231> <t232>When I entered the living room, she was already in her chair, her left foot on the footstool with its petit-point cushion embroidered with a basket of roses.</t232> <t233>She had the knitting on the floor, next to the chair, and the needles stuck into it.</t233>
<t234>I stood in front of her, with my hands crossed.</t234> <t235>All right, she said.</t235> <t236>She took a cigarette and placed it between her lips to light it.</t236> <t237>While holding it, her lips looked thin, framed by those vertical lines you see on the lips in cosmetic ads.</t237> <t238>The lighter was ivory-colored.</t238> <t239>The cigarettes must have been from the black market, I thought, which gave me hope.</t239> <t240>Even now that there's no real money, there is a black market.</t240> <t241>There is always a black market, there is always something that can be traded.</t241> <t242>She was a woman who could bend the rules.</t242> <t243>But what did I have to trade?</t243>
<t244>I looked at the cigarette with longing.</t244> <t245>For me, like alcoholic drinks and coffee, cigarettes are forbidden.</t245>
<t246>So that old fellow didn't work out, he said.</t246>
<t247>No, ma'am, I replied.</t247>
<t248>He let out something like a laugh and then coughed.</t248> <t249>Bad luck for you, he said.</t249> <t250>It's the second one, isn't it?</t250>
<t251>The third one, ma'am, I said.</t251>
<t252>And yours, he added.</t252> <t253>Another laugh and he coughed again.</t253> <t254>You can sit down.</t254> <t255>Don't make it a habit, it's just this once.</t255>
<t256>I sat on the edge of one of the straight-backed chairs.</t256> <t257>I didn't want to keep my gaze fixed or give the impression that I was distracted; so the marble shelf to my right, the mirror above it, and the bouquets of flowers were just shadows I caught out of the corner of my eye.</t257> <t258>Later on, I would have plenty of time to look at them.</t258>
<t259>Now her face was at the same height as mine.</t259> <t260>She seemed familiar to me, or at least I saw something familiar in her.</t260> <t261>A bit of her hair could be seen beneath the veil.</t261> <t262>It was still blonde.</t262> <t263>Then I thought that maybe she dyed it, that hair dye could be another one of the things she got on the black market, but now I know it's truly blonde.</t263> <t264>She had her eyebrows plucked into thin arched lines, which gave her a look of permanent surprise, or grievance, or inquisitiveness, like that of a frightened child, but her eyelids had a tired expression.</t264> <t265>Not so her eyes, a hostile blue like a midsummer sky in which the sun shines, an implacable blue.</t265>
<t266>At one time, her nose must have been pretty, but now it was too small in relation to her face, which wasn't fat but was large.</t266> <t267>From the corners of her lips, two descending lines emerged, and between them, her chin protruded, clenched as if it were a fist.</t267>
<t268>I want to see you as little as possible, he said.</t268> <t269>I hope you feel the same way about me.</t269>
<t270>I didn't respond: a yes could have been insulting, and a no, defiant.</t270>
<t271>I know you're not stupid, he continued.</t271> <t272>He took a drag and exhaled a puff of smoke.</t272>
<t273>I have read your file.</t273> <t274>As far as I am concerned, this is like a business transaction.</t274>
<t275>But if you cause me trouble, the problem will be yours.</t275>
<t276>Understood?</t276>
<t277>Yes, ma'am, I said.</t277>
<t278>And don't call me ma'am, she warned me irritably.</t278> <t279>You're not a Martha.</t279>
<t280>I didn't ask her how I was supposed to call her, because I realized that she trusted I wouldn't have the occasion to call her in any way.</t280> <t281>I felt disappointed.</t281> <t282>I had wished that she would become my older sister, a maternal figure, someone who would understand and protect me.</t282> <t283>The Wife of the detachment from which I came spent most of her time in her room; the Marthas said she drank.</t283> <t284>I wanted this one to be different.</t284> <t285>I wanted to believe that she had liked me, in another time and place, in another life.</t285> <t286>But I soon realized that she didn't like me, nor did I like her.</t286>
<t287>He put out the cigarette, without finishing it, in a small ashtray with scrolls on a little table that was beside him.</t287> <t288>He did it with a resolute attitude, giving it a sharp tap and then crushing it, instead of putting it out with a series of delicate taps, as almost all the other Wives were accustomed to doing.</t288>
<t289>As for my husband, she said, that's exactly what he is: my husband.</t289> <t290>I want this to be absolutely clear.</t290> <t291>Until death do us part.</t291> <t292>And that's it.</t292>
<t293>Yes, ma'am, I said again, forgetting her previous warning.</t293> <t294>In the past, little girls had dolls that talked when a string on their back was pulled; I had the impression that she spoke like one of them, with a monotonous voice, a doll's voice.</t294> <t295>Surely she fervently wished to slap me.</t295> <t296>They can punish us, there is a biblical precedent.</t296> <t297>But they cannot use any instruments; only their hands.</t297>
<t298>This is one of the things we fight for, said the Commander's Wife, and I noticed that she was not looking at me but at her knobby, diamond-laden hands; then I understood where I had seen her before.</t298>
<t299>The first time was on television, when I was eight or nine years old.</t299> <t300>On Sunday mornings, my mother would stay in bed sleeping, and I would get up early and sit in front of the television set in her study, clumsily switching from one channel to another, looking for cartoons.</t300> <t301>Sometimes, if I couldn't find them, I would watch The Hour of the Gospel for Innocent Souls, where they told biblical stories for children and sang hymns.</t301> <t302>One of the women was named Serena Joy.</t302> <t303>She was the soprano and main character, a petite woman with ash-blonde hair, a turned-up nose, and blue eyes that always looked up at the sky during the hymns.</t303> <t304>She was capable of laughing and crying at the same time, gracefully letting one or two tears slide down her cheeks, as if it were something rehearsed, while her voice rose with the highest notes, trembling, without any effort.</t304> <t305>It was later that she dedicated herself to other things.</t305>
<t306>The woman who was sitting in front of me was Serena Joy.</t306> <t307>Or she had been once.</t307> <t308>This was worse than I thought.</t308>
<t309>CHAPTER 4</t309>
<t310>I walk along the gravel path that cleanly divides the lawn as if it were a part in the hair.</t310> <t311>It rained last night: the grass is wet and the air is humid.</t311>
<t312>Everywhere there are worms —evidence of the soil's fertility— that have been caught by the sun, half-dead, flexible and pink, like lips.</t312>
<t313>I open the white picket gate, walk past the front lawn, and head towards the main entrance.</t313> <t314>One of the Guardians assigned to our house is washing the car in the driveway.</t314> <t315>That means the Commander is in the house, in his rooms on the other side of the dining room, where he seems to spend most of his time.</t315>
<t316>It's a very expensive car, a Whirlwind; better than a Chariot, much better than the heavy and practical Behemoth.</t316> <t317>It's black, of course the color of prestige —and of hearses— and long and elegant.</t317> <t318>The driver lovingly rubs it with a chamois.</t318> <t319>At least one thing hasn't changed: the way men take care of good cars.</t319>
<t320>He is wearing the Guardians' uniform, but he has the cap tilted jauntily and the shirt rolled up to the elbows, exposing his tanned forearms shaded by dark hair.</t320> <t321>He has a cigarette hooked in the corner of his lips, which shows that he also has something he can trade in the black market.</t321>
<t322>I know his name is Nick.</t322> <t323>I know because I heard Rita and Cora talking about him, and once I heard the Commander say to him: Nick, I won't need the car.</t323>
<t324>He lives here, in the house, above the garage.</t324> <t325>He belongs to a lower social class; he hasn't been assigned a woman, not even one.</t325> <t326>He doesn't meet the conditions: some defect, or lack of connections.</t326> <t327>But he acts as if he doesn't know or doesn't care.</t327> <t328>He is very carefree and not servile enough.</t328> <t329>It could be due to stupidity, but I don't think so.</t329>
<t330>They used to say that his behavior smelled fishy, or that it was suspicious.</t330> <t331>He is not well regarded because he is a misfit.</t331> <t332>Despite myself, I imagine how he must smell: not fishy, but like tanned skin, damp under the sun and infused with cigarette smoke.</t332> <t333>I sigh just thinking about it.</t333>
<t334>He looks at me and sees that I look at him.</t334> <t335>He has a Latino face, thin, angular, and wrinkles around his mouth from so much smiling.</t335> <t336>He takes one last drag on the cigarette, drops it to the ground, and steps on it.</t336> <t337>He starts to whistle and winks at me.</t337>
<t338>I lower my head, turn in such a way that the white hood hides my face, and start walking.</t338> <t339>He has taken the risk, but for what?</t339> <t340>And if I tried to betray him?</t340>
<t341>Maybe he just wanted to appear friendly.</t341> <t342>Maybe he saw my expression and misunderstood it.</t342> <t343>Actually, what I wanted was the cigarette.</t343>
<t344>Maybe he did it to test, to see my reaction.</t344>
<t345>Maybe it's a spy.</t345>
<t346>I open the main gate and close it behind me.</t346> <t347>I look down, but not back.</t347> <t348>The sidewalk is made of red bricks.</t348> <t349>I fix my gaze on the ground, a field of rectangles that form gentle waves where the earth, after decades and decades of winter frosts, has become warped.</t349> <t350>The color of the bricks is old, but fresh and clean.</t350> <t351>The sidewalks are kept cleaner than they used to be in the past.</t351>
<t352>I walk to the corner and wait.</t352> <t353>I couldn't stand waiting before.</t353> <t354>You can also serve simply by waiting, Aunt Lydia used to say.</t354> <t355>She made us memorize it.</t355>
<t356>He also said: Not all of you will overcome it.</t356> <t357>Some of you will fail or encounter obstacles.</t357> <t358>Some of you are weak.</t358> <t359>He had a mole on his chin that moved up and down as he spoke.</t359> <t360>He said: Imagine you are seeds, and immediately adopted a fawning and conspiratorial tone, like ballet teachers when they said to the children:</t360>
<t361>Now let's raise our arms... let's imagine that we are trees.</t361>
<t362>I am standing on the corner, pretending to be a tree.</t362>
<t363>A red figure with a face framed by a white bonnet, a figure like mine, an unremarkable woman, with a basket, walking towards me on the red brick sidewalk.</t363> <t364>She stops beside me and we look at each other's faces through the white tunnel that serves as our frame.</t364> <t365>She is the one I was waiting for.</t365>
<t366>—Blessed be the fruit —she says to me, pronouncing the accepted greeting among us.</t366>
<t367>—May the Lord allow it to mature —I recite the accepted response.</t367>
<t368>We turn and pass by the houses, heading towards the city center.</t368> <t369>We are not allowed to go there, except in pairs.</t369> <t370>It is supposed to be for our protection, although it is an absurd idea: we are already well protected.</t370> <t371>The reality is that she is my spy, and I am hers.</t371> <t372>If either of us makes a slip during one of our daily walks, the other bears the responsibility.</t372>
<t373>This woman has been my companion for the past two weeks.</t373> <t374>I don't know what happened to the previous one.</t374> <t375>One day she simply didn't show up, and this one was in her place.</t375> <t376>You don't ask questions about these kinds of things, because the answers are usually unpleasant.</t376> <t377>Anyway, there wouldn't be an answer either.</t377>
<t378>This one is a little chubbier than me.</t378> <t379>She has brown eyes.</t379> <t380>Her name is Deglen, and those are the two or three things I know about her.</t380> <t381>She walks demurely, with her head down, her hands in red gloves crossed in front, and with short steps, like those a pig trained to walk on its hind legs would take.</t381> <t382>During the walks, she has never said anything that is not strictly orthodox, so neither have I.</t382> <t383>She must be a true believer; in her case, being a Handmaid must be more than just a name.</t383> <t384>So I can't take the risk.</t384>
<t385>—I have heard that the war is going well —he comments.</t385>
<t386>—Praise be —I respond.</t386>
<t387>—We've had good weather.</t387>
<t388>—Which fills me with joy.</t388>
<t389>—Since yesterday, they have defeated more groups of rebels.</t389>
<t390>—Praise be —I say.</t390> <t391>I don't ask how you know—.</t391> <t392>What were they?</t392>
<t393>—Baptists.</t393> <t394>They had a fortress in the Blue Mountains.</t394> <t395>But they were forced to evacuate it with smoke bombs.</t395>
<t396>—Praise be.</t396>
<t397>Sometimes I wish he would shut up and let me walk in peace.</t397> <t398>But I am hungry for news, any kind of news; even if they were false, they would still mean something.</t398>
<t399>We arrived at the first barrier, which is like the ones used to block the way when doing construction work, or to lift manhole covers: a wooden cross painted with yellow and black stripes and a red hexagon that means Stop.</t399> <t400>Near the door, there are some lanterns that are off because it hasn't gotten dark yet.</t400> <t401>I know that above our heads there are lights attached to the telephone poles, which are used in emergencies; and that in the bunkers on both sides of the road, there are men stationed with machine guns.</t401> <t402>The headscarf that surrounds my face prevents me from seeing the lights and the bunkers.</t402> <t403>But I know they are there.</t403>
<t404>Behind the barrier, next to the narrow entrance, two men dressed in the green uniform of the Guardians of the Faith await us, with plumes on their epaulettes and a beret that features two crossed swords above a white triangle.</t404> <t405>The Guardians are not real soldiers.</t405> <t406>They are assigned guard duties and other menial tasks, such as digging the soil in the Commander's Wife's garden, and they are either stupid, old, invalid, or very young; and then there are the undercover Spies.</t406>
<t407>These two are very young: one of them still has a sparse mustache and the other a red face.</t407> <t408>Their youth is touching, but I know I must not deceive myself.</t408> <t409>Youths are often the most dangerous, the most fanatical, and the ones who get the most agitated when they have a weapon in their hands.</t409> <t410>They do not yet possess experience.</t410> <t411>One must be very tactful with them.</t411>
<t412>Last week, right here, they shot a woman.</t412> <t413>She was a Martha.</t413>
<t414>He was rummaging through his suit, looking for the pass, and they thought he was going to pull out a bomb.</t414> <t415>They took her for a man in disguise.</t415> <t416>There have been several incidents of this kind.</t416>
<t417>Rita and Cora knew that woman.</t417> <t418>I heard them talking about her in the kitchen.</t418>
<t419>They fulfilled their obligation, said Cora.</t419> <t420>To ensure our safety.</t420>
<t421>There is nothing more certain than death, said Rita angrily.</t421> <t422>She didn't bother anyone.</t422> <t423>There was no reason to shoot her.</t423>
<t424>It was an accident, replied Cora.</t424>
<t425>None of that, protested Rita.</t425> <t426>All of this is unpleasant.</t426> <t427>I could hear her moving the pots in the sink.</t427>
<t428>Well, anyway, they would think twice before blowing up this house, Cora stated.</t428>
<t429>It doesn't matter, replied Rita.</t429> <t430>She was very hardworking.</t430> <t431>She didn't deserve to die like that.</t431>
<t432>There are worse deaths, commented Cora.</t432> <t433>At least this one was quick.</t433>
<t434>You can say it, concluded Rita.</t434> <t435>I would prefer to have a little time.</t435> <t436>To fix things.</t436>
<t437>The two young Guardians greet us by bringing three fingers to the edge of their berets.</t437>
<t438>That is the signal for us.</t438> <t439>They are supposed to show us respect, due to the nature of our mission.</t439>
<t440>We take our passes out of the zippered pockets of our wide sleeves; they inspect and stamp them.</t440> <t441>One of the young men enters the fort on the right to punch the numbers on our passes with the Compuchec.</t441>
<t442>When he returns my pass, the man with the peach-colored mustache tilts his head, trying to get a look at my face.</t442> <t443>I lift my head a bit to help him; he looks into my eyes, I look into his, and he blushes.</t443> <t444>His face is long and sad, like that of a lamb, and he has large, deep eyes, like those of a dog... a spaniel, not a terrier.</t444> <t445>His skin is white and seems unhealthily fragile, like the skin beneath a scab.</t445> <t446>However, I imagine placing my hand on this exposed face.</t446> <t447>He is the one who pulls away.</t447>
<t448>This is an event, a small challenge to the norms, so brief that it may go unnoticed; but these moments are a reward that I reserve for myself, like the candy that, as a child, I hid in the back of a drawer.</t448>
<t449>Moments like this are an opportunity that opens up, like a tiny peephole.</t449>
<t450>And what if he came at night, when he is alone —although they would never allow him to be so alone—, and let him go beyond my veil?</t450> <t451>And what if I took off my red veil and exhibited myself before him, before them, under the uncertain light of the street lamps?</t451> <t452>This is what they must sometimes think, as they spend the dead hours behind this barrier that no one ever crosses except the Commanders of the Faith in their long, purring black cars, or their blue Wives, and their daughters with their white veils on their devout journey to Salvation or Prayvaganzas, or their plump green Marthas, or an occasional Birthmobile, or their red Handmaids, on foot.</t452> <t453>Or, sometimes, a van painted black, with the white eye on the side.</t453> <t454>The windows of the vans are dark, and the men in the front seat wear dark glasses: one darkness over another.</t454>
<t455>By the way, the vans are quieter than the rest of the cars.</t455> <t456>When they pass by, we look away.</t456> <t457>If any sound comes from inside, we try not to hear it.</t457>
<t458>Out of sight, out of mind.</t458>
<t459>When the vans arrive at a checkpoint, they are signaled to pass without stopping.</t459> <t460>The Guardians do not want to risk inspecting the interior and questioning their authority.</t460> <t461>Regardless of what they think.</t461>
<t462>If they think at all, although it's impossible to tell from their expression.</t462>
<t463>The most likely thing is that they don't think about anything promiscuous.</t463> <t464>If they think about a kiss, they must immediately think about the lights turning on and the rifle shots.</t464> <t465>In reality, they think about doing their job, about ascending to the category of Angels, maybe about being allowed to marry and, if they are capable of attaining enough power and reach old age, about being assigned a Handmaid just for them.</t465>
<t466>The man with the mustache opens the small gate for pedestrians, steps back to make room for us, and we pass through.</t466> <t467>I know that as we move forward, these two men—who are still not allowed to touch women—are watching us.</t467> <t468>However, they touch us with their eyes and I sway my hips a little and feel the swing of my wide skirt.</t468> <t469>It's like mocking someone from the other side of the fence, or teasing a dog with a bone by keeping it out of reach, and I immediately feel ashamed of my behavior because none of this is the fault of those men, they are too young.</t469>
<t470>Soon I discover that I am not actually ashamed.</t470> <t471>I enjoy the power: the power of a bone, which does nothing but is there.</t471> <t472>I harbor the hope that they will have a hard time looking at us and will have to rub against the barriers, surreptitiously.</t472> <t473>And</t473>
<t474>that later, at night, they suffer on the regiment's cots.</t474> <t475>Now they have no relief except their own bodies, and that is a sacrilege.</t475> <t476>There are no more magazines, no movies, no substitutes; only me and my shadow moving away from the two men, who stand rigidly by the barricade while watching our figures.</t476>
<t477>CHAPTER 5</t477>
<t478>I walk down the street accompanied by my double.</t478> <t479>Although we are no longer in the enclosed area of the Commanders, there are also huge houses here.</t479> <t480>In one of them, a Guardian can be seen mowing the lawn.</t480> <t481>The gardens are well-kept, the facades are beautiful and well-preserved; they are like those beautiful pictures that used to appear in house and garden magazines and interior design magazines.</t481> <t482>And the same absence of people, the same feeling that everything is asleep.</t482> <t483>The street is almost like a museum, as if it were part of a model of a city, made to show how people lived.</t483> <t484>And just like in those photos, those museums and those models, not a single child can be seen.</t484>
<t485>We are in the center of Gilead, where the war only reaches us through the television.</t485> <t486>We are not sure where the boundaries are; they vary according to the attacks and counterattacks.</t486> <t487>But this is the center, and here nothing moves.</t487> <t488>The Republic of Gilead, Aunt Lydia said, has no borders.</t488> <t489>Gilead is within you.</t489>
<t490>Once upon a time, doctors, lawyers, and university professors lived here.</t490> <t491>But now there are no more lawyers, and the universities are closed.</t491>
<t492>Sometimes, Luke and I would walk together through these streets.</t492> <t493>We would say that we would buy a house like this one, a big house, and that we would fix it up.</t493>
<t494>We would have a garden and swings for the children.</t494> <t495>Because we would have children.</t495> <t496>Even though we knew it was not very likely that we could afford such a luxury, at least it was a topic of conversation, a game for Sundays.</t496> <t497>Now, that freedom seems like a chimera.</t497>
<t498>At the corner, we turn onto the main street, where there is more traffic.</t498> <t499>Cars pass by, most of them black, and some gray or brown.</t499> <t500>There are other women with baskets, some dressed in red, others in the dull green of the Marthas, others in dresses with red, blue, and green stripes, cheap and modest, proof that they are the wives of the poorest men.</t500> <t501>They are called econowives.</t501> <t502>These women are not divided according to their functions, they have to do everything, if they can.</t502> <t503>Occasionally, you see a woman completely dressed in black, which means she is a widow.</t503>
<t504>There used to be more widows seen, but they seem to be disappearing.</t504>
<t505>You don't see Commanders' Wives on the sidewalks: they only ride in cars.</t505>
<t506>Here, the sidewalks are made of cement.</t506> <t507>I try not to step on the cracks, like children do.</t507>
<t508>I remember when I used to walk on these sidewalks, in different times, and the footwear I used to wear.</t508> <t509>Sometimes I wore running shoes with padded insides and little holes for the foot to breathe, and fluorescent fabric stars that reflected light in the dark.</t509> <t510>However, I never ran at night, and during the day I only did so on very crowded streets.</t510> <t511>Back then, women were not protected.</t511>
<t512>I remember the rules, rules that were not written, but that any woman knew: do not open the door to a stranger, even if he says he is a policeman; in that case, tell him to pass his identification card under the door.</t512> <t513>Do not stop on the road to help a motorist who seems to have a problem; do not brake and keep going.</t513> <t514>If someone gets in, do not turn around to look.</t514> <t515>Do not enter a laundromat alone at night.</t515>
<t516>I think about the laundromats.</t516> <t517>I think about what I used to wear to go: shorts, jeans, or tracksuit.</t517> <t518>And what I put in the washing machine: my own clothes, my own detergent, my own money, the money I had earned.</t518> <t519>I remember what it was like to keep track of the money.</t519>
<t520>Now we walk down the same street, two by two and in red, and no man shouts obscenities at us, nor speaks to us, nor touches us.</t520> <t521>No one whistles at us.</t521>
<t522>There is more than one kind of freedom, Aunt Lydia used to say.</t522> <t523>Freedom to and freedom from.</t523> <t524>In the days of anarchy, it was freedom to.</t524> <t525>Now we are given freedom from.</t525> <t526>Do not underrate it.</t526>
<t527>In front of us, to the right, is the shop where we ordered the dresses.</t527> <t528>Some people call them habits, a good definition: it's hard to give up habits.</t528>
<t529>On the store's facade, there is a huge wooden sign in the shape of a lily: it is called Wild Lilies.</t529> <t530>Below the lily, you can see the spot where the label was painted; but they decided that even the names of the stores were too much temptation for us.</t530> <t531>Now the stores are known only by the signs.</t531>
<t532>Before, Azucenas was a cinema.</t532> <t533>It was very popular among students; every spring the Humphrey Bogart festival was celebrated, with Lauren Bacall or Katherine Hepburn, independent and determined women.</t533> <t534>They wore buttoned blouses that suggested the various possibilities of the word loose.</t534> <t535>Those women could be Loose; or not.</t535> <t536>They seemed capable of choosing.</t536> <t537>In those times we seemed capable of choosing.</t537> <t538>We are a Society in decline, Aunt Lydia said, with too many choices.</t538>
<t539>I don't know when they stopped celebrating the festival.</t539> <t540>Surely I had already grown up.</t540>
<t541>That's why I didn't find out.</t541>
<t542>We didn't enter Azucenas; we crossed the street and walked along the sidewalk.</t542> <t543>The first place we entered was a store that also had a wooden sign: three eggs, a bee, and a cow.</t543> <t544>Milk and honey.</t544> <t545>There was a line; we joined it to wait our turn, always two by two.</t545> <t546>I saw that today they had oranges.</t546> <t547>Since Central America was lost to the Libertos, oranges have been hard to come by: sometimes they have them and sometimes they don't.</t547> <t548>Because of the war, not many oranges come from California either, and you can't count on the ones from Florida because of the barricades and the blowing up of the railroad tracks.</t548> <t549>I looked at the oranges and my mouth watered.</t549> <t550>But I hadn't brought any vouchers for oranges.</t550> <t551>It occurred to me that I could go back and tell Rita.</t551> <t552>She would love it.</t552> <t553>Showing up with the oranges would be a small triumph.</t553>
<t554>As they reach the counter, the women hand over their vouchers to the two men in Guardian uniforms, who are on the other side.</t554> <t555>Practically no one speaks, but there is a murmur and the women furtively move their heads, looking from side to side.</t555> <t556>It is at these moments, while shopping, that you might see someone you know from the old days, or from the Red Center.</t556> <t557>Just the sight of one of those faces would be exhilarating.</t557> <t558>If I could see Moira, just see her, know that she still exists...</t558> <t559>Now it is hard to remember what it means to have a friend.</t559>
<t560>But Deglen, who is next to me, does not look.</t560> <t561>Perhaps she no longer knows anyone.</t561> <t562>Maybe all the women she knew have disappeared.</t562> <t563>Maybe she doesn't want to be seen.</t563>
<t564>Remain silent, with your head down.</t564>
<t565>While we wait in double file, the door opens and two other women enter, both dressed in red and wearing the white bonnet of the Handmaids.</t565> <t566>One of them is pregnant; her belly, under the loose clothes, protrudes triumphantly.</t566> <t567>There is a stir in the room, a whisper is heard, some sighs; much to our dismay, we turn our heads shamelessly to get a better look.</t567> <t568>We feel an enormous desire to touch her.</t568> <t569>To us, she is a magical presence, an object of envy and desire, of covetousness.</t569> <t570>She is like a flag on the top of a mountain, the proof that something can still be done: we too can save ourselves.</t570>
<t571>The excitement is such that the women whisper, almost converse.</t571>
<t572>—Who is it? —I hear them ask behind me.</t572>
<t573>—Dewayne.</t573> <t574>No. Dewarren.</t574>
<t575>—How she shows off —someone murmurs, and it's true.</t575> <t576>A pregnant woman is not obligated to go out or go shopping.</t576> <t577>The daily walk is no longer mandatory, to maintain the proper functioning of her abdominal muscles.</t577> <t578>She only needs normal exercises and breathing exercises.</t578> <t579>She could stay at home.</t579> <t580>In reality, it is dangerous for her to go out, and there is always a Guardian waiting for her by the door.</t580>
<t581>Now that she is carrying a new life, she is closer to death and needs special protection.</t581> <t582>She could become jealous, which has happened in other cases.</t582>
<t583>Now all children are wanted, but not by everyone.</t583>
<t584>But the walk can be a craving, and if there hasn't been a miscarriage and the pregnancy has reached this point, they like to satisfy the cravings.</t584> <t585>Or maybe she is one of those who love to say: Make a pile, and I'll take it, in other words, a martyr.</t585> <t586>She looks around and I manage to see her face.</t586> <t587>The one who was murmuring was right: she has come to show off; she is brimming with health and enjoys every minute.</t587>
<t588>—Silence —says one of the Guardians from behind the counter, and we fall silent like schoolgirls.</t588>
<t589>Deglen and I have reached the counter.</t589> <t590>We hand over the vouchers and one of the Guardians records a number on them with the Compuperfo, while the other gives us our purchase, the milk and the eggs.</t590> <t591>We put them in our baskets and go back out, passing by the pregnant woman and her companion who, compared to the first, looks scrawny and wrinkled... just like all of us.</t591> <t592>The belly of a pregnant woman is like an immense fruit.</t592> <t593>Somoflafla, a word from my childhood.</t593>
<t594>She rests her hands on him, as if she wanted to defend him, or as if she were seeking warmth and strength within herself.</t594>
<t595>When I pass by, she looks me directly in the eyes, and then I recognize her.</t595> <t596>She was with me in the Red Center, she was one of Aunt Lydia's favorites.</t596> <t597>I never liked her.</t597>
<t598>In those times, her name was Janine.</t598>
<t599>Janine looks at me and an affected smile appears at the corners of her lips.</t599> <t600>She lowers her gaze to my belly—a board beneath the red suit—and touches it, covering her face.</t600>
<t601>I can only see a small piece of his forehead and the pink tip of his nose.</t601>
<t602>Then we enter All Meat, marked with a huge pork chop hanging from two chains.</t602> <t603>There isn't much of a line here: meat is expensive and even Commanders can't eat it every day.</t603> <t604>However —and this is the second time this week—, Deglen picks up steaks.</t604> <t605>I'll tell the Marthas: this is the kind of comment they love to hear.</t605> <t606>They are extremely interested in knowing how the other houses manage; these trivial gossip give them the opportunity to feel proud or disgusted.</t606>
<t607>I grab the chicken, wrapped in paraffin paper and tied with a string.</t607> <t608>There aren't many plastic things left anymore.</t608> <t609>I remember those white plastic bags they used to give out at supermarkets; how I hated wasting them, I would pile them up under the sink until there came a point when there were so many that when you opened the cabinet door, they would slip to the floor.</t609> <t610>Luke used to complain and every now and then he would take them all out and throw them away.</t610>
<t611>She could grab one and put it on her head, she warned me.</t611> <t612>You know the things children do when they play.</t612> <t613>She would never do that, I told her.</t613> <t614>She's grown up now.</t614> <t615>(Or</t615>
<t616>intelligent, or lucky.)</t616> <t617>But she felt a chill, and then guilt for having been so reckless.</t617> <t618>It was true, I took everything for granted, in those days I relied on luck.</t618> <t619>I'll store them in a higher cabinet, she said.</t619> <t620>Don't store them, Luke repeated.</t620>
<t621>We never used them.</t621> <t622>As garbage bags, I insisted, and he would tell me...</t622>
<t623>Not here.</t623> <t624>People are watching.</t624> <t625>I turn around and see my silhouette in the store window.</t625> <t626>So we went out, we are on the street...</t626>
<t627>A group of people approaches us.</t627> <t628>They are tourists, they seem to be from Japan, perhaps part of a commercial delegation, and they are visiting historical sites or admiring the local color.</t628> <t629>They are small and neatly dressed.</t629> <t630>Each one carries a camera and a smile.</t630> <t631>They observe everything attentively, tilting their heads to one side, like robins; their joy is aggressive and I can't stand looking at them.</t631>
<t632>It had been a long time since I had seen women with skirts like these.</t632> <t633>They reach exactly below the knees, and beneath the skirts, their almost bare legs can be seen with those fine and striking stockings, and the high-heeled shoes with straps clinging to their feet like delicate instruments of torture.</t633> <t634>They sway, as if their feet were nailed to uneven stilts; their backs are arched at the waist and their buttocks are prominent.</t634> <t635>They wear their heads uncovered and their hair loose in all its darkness and sexuality; their lips painted red, outlining the moist cavities of their mouths like the scribbles on the wall of a public restroom from another time.</t635>

<t724>The Wall is also hundreds of years old, or at least more than a century.</t724> <t725>Like the sidewalks, it is made of red bricks, and it must have once been simple, yet beautiful.</t725> <t726>Now the gates are guarded by sentinels, and above them there are horrible spotlights mounted on metal poles, barbed wire at the bottom, and shards of glass at the top.</t726>
